8
Finding Optimal Biological Dose
8.1
Introduction
Conventionally, the primary objective of phase I oncology trials is to establish
the maximum tolerated dose (MTD). This more-is-better paradigm is based
on the monotonicity assumption that a higher dose leads to higher toxicity and
also higher eÔ¨Écacy, which typically holds for conventional chemotherapies.
The advent of novel targeted therapy and immunotherapy, such as check-
point inhibitors and chimeric antigen receptor (CAR) T-cell therapy, has rev-
olutionized cancer treatment. For these novel therapies, although toxicity typ-
ically increases with the dose, eÔ¨Écacy may plateau or even decrease at high
doses (Cook et al., 2015; Sachs et al., 2016; Shah et al., 2021). In addition,
some targeted therapies demonstrate minimal toxicity in the therapeutic dose
range, making MTD unlikely to be reached (Mathijssen et al., 2014). In these
cases, the conventional more-is-better paradigm, ignoring eÔ¨Écacy data, does
not depict the underlying setting and may result in undesirable consequences.
(Shah et al., 2021). As a result, FDA Oncology Center of Excellence recently
initiated Project Optimus ‚Äúto reform the dose optimization and dose selection
paradigm in oncology drug development‚Äù (FDA, 2022).
To illustrate the issue, consider Ô¨Åve doses of a targeted or immunother-
apy agent, d1 < ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ < d5. Suppose that the true toxicity probabilities for the
Ô¨Åve doses are (œÄT 1, . . . , œÄT 5) = (0.05, 0.12, 0.27, 0.35, 0.50). If the true eÔ¨Écacy
probabilities are (œÄE1, . . . , œÄE5) = (0.20, 0.35, 0.36, 0.37, 0.38), then eÔ¨Écacy
reaches a plateau of about 0.35 at dose d2. All dose-Ô¨Ånding methods consider-
ing toxicity only and with a target toxicity probability of 0.3 or 0.25, are most
likely to select d3 as MTD and use it for cohort expansion or a subsequent
phase II trial. However, d2 is obviously more desirable than MTD (i.e., d3)
with much lower toxicity and virtually identical eÔ¨Écacy. Any ‚Äútoxicity-only‚Äù
phase I method cannot determine this because it ignores eÔ¨Écacy.
Furthermore, suppose that the toxicity rates are the same but the true eÔ¨É-
cacy probabilities are (œÄE1, . . . , œÄE5) = (0.01, 0.05, 0.30, 0.60, 0.60). Escalating
from d3 to d4 only increases the toxicity probability from 0.27 to 0.35, but
doubles the eÔ¨Écacy probability from 0.3 to 0.6. Often this small increase in
toxicity may be considered as a reasonable trade-oÔ¨Äfor the large increase in
eÔ¨Écacy by choosing d4 rather than d3, however, toxicity-only methods cannot
determine this.
DOI: 10.1201/9780429052781-8
155

156
Model-Assisted Bayesian Designs for Dose Finding and Optimization
Lastly, if the agent is ineÔ¨Äective for all doses, with true eÔ¨Écacy probabilities
(œÄE1, . . . , œÄE5) = (0.00, 0.01, 0.01, 0.02, 0.02), the best decision is to not choose
any dose, but the toxicity-based methods still are most likely to choose d3.
These examples demonstrate the deÔ¨Åciency of choosing a ‚Äúbest‚Äù dose based
only on toxicity (e.g., MTD), ignoring eÔ¨Écacy, which may lead to the failure
of subsequent phase II or III trials.
8.1.1
Phase I‚ÄìII design paradigm
The aforementioned issues can be avoided by adopting the phase I‚ÄìII design
paradigm, which considers both toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy to adaptively allocate
patients and identify the optimal biological dose (OBD). In general, the OBD
is deÔ¨Åned as the dose that optimizes a certain metric of the risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-
oÔ¨Ä. Figure 8.1 shows the schema of phase I‚ÄìII designs, which consist of the
following elements:
‚Ä¢ Toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy outcomes that characterize potential risks and
beneÔ¨Åts of the treatment being investigated. Examples of toxicity outcome
include dose limiting toxicity (DLT) or tolerability rates, and examples of ef-
Ô¨Åcacy outcome include tumor responses, pharmacodynamic (PD) biomarkers
measuring biological activities of the drug (e.g., inhibition of an oncogene
pathway or proliferation of T cells), or surrogate eÔ¨Écacy endpoints (e.g.,
ctDNA, minimal residual disease). In this chapter, when describing designs,
we use the toxicity rate and eÔ¨Écacy rate to generically represent the risk and
beneÔ¨Åt of the treatment, respectively, which should be customized based on
the trial under consideration.
‚Ä¢ Risk‚ÄìbeneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Äcriterion that characterizes and quantiÔ¨Åes the
trade-oÔ¨Äbetween eÔ¨Écacy and toxicity for each dose of the drug. SpeciÔ¨Åc
example includes the trade-oÔ¨Äbased on marginal toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy prob-
abilities (Section 8.2.1) or utility based on the values of toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy
endpoints (Section 8.3.1).
‚Ä¢ Optimal biological dose (OBD) that maximizes the predeÔ¨Åned risk‚Äì
beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Äcriterion among all considered doses.
‚Ä¢ Statistical model that describes the relationship between dose, toxicity,
and eÔ¨Écacy. Examples include the Gumbel-Morgenstern copula model (Sec-
tion 8.2.2) and multinomial model for bivariate binary toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy
endpoints (Section 8.3.2).
‚Ä¢ Adaptive decision rule that determines the best dose for the next cohort,
based on the (dose, toxicity, eÔ¨Écacy) data observed from previous patients.
Typically, the next cohort is assigned to the dose that has the highest esti-
mate of the risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Ä, based on the interim data.
‚Ä¢ Admissibility rules that protect patients in the trial from unacceptably
toxic or ineÔ¨Écacious doses. Admissibility rules are used to deÔ¨Åne admissible
doses that satisfy certain prespeciÔ¨Åed toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy requirements.

Finding Optimal Biological Dose
157
Upon identiÔ¨Åcation, only admissible doses can be used to treat patients.
Examples will be provided in the next section.
‚Ä¢ Stopping rule that terminates the trial early if all the doses being consid-
ered are unacceptably toxic or ineÔ¨Écacious.
‚Ä¢ Selection rule that identiÔ¨Åes OBD for subsequent studies if the trial is not
terminated early. Typically, OBD is selected as the dose that is admissible
and has the most favorable estimate of the risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Ä.
A number of phase I‚ÄìII designs have been proposed in recent years. The
majority of them are model-based. These model-based designs consider diÔ¨Äer-
ent settings (e.g., diÔ¨Äerent types of endpoints, statistical models, risk-beneÔ¨Åt
trade-oÔ¨Äcriteria, and decision rules), but share a similar design strategy to
that outlined in Figure 8.1. Thall and Cook (2004) introduced a toxicity‚Äì
eÔ¨Écacy trade-oÔ¨Äcontour for dose Ô¨Ånding based on the Gumbel-Morgenstern
copula model (hereafter referred to as the EÔ¨ÄTox design). Bekele and Shen
(2005) considered jointly modeling a binary toxicity outcome and a continuous
eÔ¨Écacy outcome in phase I‚ÄìII dose-Ô¨Ånding oncology trials. Hunsberger et al.
(2005) proposed a slope-sign design to guide dose escalation. Yin et al. (2006)
parameterized both the toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy probabilities based on multivari-
ate normal random variables, and they applied the global cross-ratio model
(Dale, 1986) to jointly quantify the toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy outcomes. Zhang et al.
(2006) developed a Ô¨Çexible continuation-ratio model to select the optimal dose
at each decision-making time. Zang et al. (2014) used the logistic model to
depict the local dose‚ÄìeÔ¨Écacy relationship around the current dose. Liu and
Johnson (2016) proposed a nonparametric design using a Ô¨Çexible Bayesian dy-
namic model for monotone dose‚Äìresponse curves. Riviere et al. (2018) utilized
adaptive randomization to determine the plateau in phase I‚ÄìII dose-Ô¨Ånding
designs. More sophisticated phase I‚ÄìII designs were developed to handle more
complex early-phase trials; for example, drug-combination trials (Yuan and

Model-Assisted Bayesian Designs for Dose Finding and Optimization
Yin, 2011a; Cai et al., 2014; Guo and Li, 2015), dose‚Äìschedule optimization
(Guo et al., 2016; Lin et al., 2020a, 2021), ordinal toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy out-
comes (Houede et al., 2010; Lee et al., 2016), personalized dose-Ô¨Ånding based
on biomarkers (Guo and Yuan, 2017), and immunotherapy considering tox-
icity, eÔ¨Écacy and immune response (Liu et al., 2018), among others. For a
comprehensive review on model-based phase I‚ÄìII designs, see Mandrekar et al.
(2010) and Yuan et al. (2016b).
These model-based phase I‚ÄìII designs are statistically complicated and
computationally intensive. To account for toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy, the model
used by the designs is signiÔ¨Åcantly more complex than that of the conven-
tional phase I trial design (e.g., CRM), as illustrated in the next section.
Highly complicated and structured parametric models also make the design
susceptible to model misspeciÔ¨Åcation. As a result, the model-based phase I-
II designs are often regarded by practitioners as diÔ¨Écult to understand and
implement, severely limiting their use in practice.
Model-assisted designs have been proposed to simplify the implementation
of phase I‚ÄìII trials, while yielding the performance comparable to or even bet-
ter than model-based designs. As model-assisted designs do not assume any
parametric dose-toxicity and -eÔ¨Écacy curves, they are also more robust than
model-based designs. Lin and Yin (2017b) developed a toxicity-eÔ¨Écacy inter-
val based phase I‚ÄìII design (called STEIN) using the pool-adjacent-violators
algorithm and model averaging. Li et al. (2017) proposed a toxicity and eÔ¨É-
cacy probability interval design that separately models toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy.
Takeda et al. (2018) presented a Bayesian optimal interval design that ac-
commodates both eÔ¨Écacy and toxicity endpoints, known as BOIN-ET. Zhou
et al. (2019) developed a two-stage utility-based Bayesian optimal interval (U-
BOIN) design using a Dirichlet-multinomial model to jointly model toxicity
and eÔ¨Écacy. Lin et al. (2020b) adopted a quasi-binomial likelihood approach
to model the observed utility directly, and developed the BOIN12 design. Shi
et al. (2021) developed a utility-based toxicity probability interval (uTPI)
design as an extension of the toxicity-only keyboard design (Yan et al., 2017).
In the following sections, we Ô¨Årst introduce the EÔ¨ÄTox design as an example
to illustrate the characteristics of model-based designs, and then we describe
several model-assisted phase I‚ÄìII designs, including the BOIN12, U-BOIN, and
uTPI designs. Other model-assisted designs such as STEIN and BOIN-ET will
be brieÔ¨Çy discussed at the end of this chapter.
8.2
EÔ¨ÄTox design
The EÔ¨ÄTox design is a model-based phase I‚ÄìII design. It considers a binary
toxicity endpoint yT and a binary eÔ¨Écacy endpoint yE, with yT = 1 and yE =
1 denoting the occurrence of toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy events, respectively. Let d1 <
¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ < dJ denote J prespeciÔ¨Åed doses. We assume that the (marginal) toxicity

Finding Optimal Biological Dose
159
probability increases with the dose, i.e., œÄT 1 < ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ < œÄT J. The (marginal)
eÔ¨Écacy probability œÄE1, ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ , œÄEJ, however, does not necessarily increase with
the dose, which may plateau or even decrease at higher doses. The EÔ¨ÄTox
design uses an eÔ¨Écacy‚Äìtoxicity trade-oÔ¨Äcontour as the criterion to deÔ¨Åne and
select OBD (Thall and Cook, 2004).
8.2.1
EÔ¨Écacy‚Äìtoxicity trade-oÔ¨Ä
To construct trade-oÔ¨Äcontours, we Ô¨Årst elicit from clinicians three equally
desirable eÔ¨Écacy‚Äìtoxicity probability pairs (œÄ(1)
E , 0), (1, œÄ(2)
T ), and (œÄ(3)

8.2.3
Admissible rules
Let œÜT be an upper limit of œÄT j and œÜE be a lower limit of œÄEj for a dose to
be acceptable, respectively. Values of œÜE and œÜT should be determined by the
clinical investigators based on the trial under consideration, and often vary
from one trial to another. For example, œÜT = 0.3 and œÜE = 0.2 means that
a dose is regarded as acceptable only when its toxicity probability ‚â§0.3 and
eÔ¨Écacy probability ‚â•0.2.
Formally, a dose dj is deÔ¨Åned as acceptable (or admissible) when it satisÔ¨Åes
the following two criteria:
(Safety criterion)
Pr(œÄT j > œÜT | Dn) ‚â§cT ,
(EÔ¨Écacy criterion)
Pr(œÄEj < œÜE | Dn) ‚â§cE,
where cE and cT are a large probability threshold such as 0.95. Let A(Dn)
denote the set of admissible doses that satisfy the above safety and eÔ¨Écacy
requirements. During the trial, A(Dn) should be continually updated based
on Dn to guide dose escalation and de-escalation. Only doses in A(Dn) can
be used to treat patients.
8.2.4
Dose-Ô¨Ånding algorithm
The dose-Ô¨Ånding algorithm of the EÔ¨ÄTox design, including the adaptive dose
assignment, stopping, and selection rules, is given as follows:

starting dose speciÔ¨Åed by the clinicians.

(4) Stop the trial when the maximum sample size N is reached. If A(DN)
is not empty, select the OBD as the dose dj‚àó‚ààA(DN) that maximizes the
eÔ¨Écacy-toxicity trade-oÔ¨Äœà(œÄEj‚àó, œÄT j‚àó).
Compared to toxicity-only dose-Ô¨Ånding designs such as CRM, by con-
sidering the eÔ¨Écacy-toxicity trade-oÔ¨Ä, the EÔ¨ÄTox design better reÔ¨Çects the
risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Äunderlying medical decisions in practice and identiÔ¨Åes
the OBD rather than MTD. In addition, the EÔ¨ÄTox design uses more data
(both toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy data) to make adaptive decisions, and thus is more
eÔ¨Écient. The challenges are that the EÔ¨ÄTox design is complicated to imple-
ment due to complex model (including prior) speciÔ¨Åcation and estimation. In
addition, the EÔ¨ÄTox design appears sensitive to model misspeciÔ¨Åcation (see
Section 8.3.5 and 8.4.4). Although we only cover the EÔ¨ÄTox design here, the
other model-based phase I‚ÄìII designs have similar structures as the EÔ¨ÄTox
design, and share similar challenges.
In the following sections, we introduce several model-assisted phase I-II
designs that are easy to implement and that yield performance comparable
or better than model-based designs such as EÔ¨ÄTox. To use the model-assisted
designs, no complicated model Ô¨Åtting is needed, and clinicians can simply
look up the decision table to determine the dose for treating the next cohort
of patients. In addition, these designs do not assume any parametric model on
the dose-toxicity and dose-eÔ¨Écacy curves, and thus are not sensitive to model
misspeciÔ¨Åcation.
8.3
U-BOIN design
U-BOIN extends the BOIN design to identify OBD for phase I-II trials (Zhou
et al., 2019). It considers a binary toxicity endpoint yT and a binary eÔ¨Écacy
endpoint yE, where yE can be tumor response or PD biomarkers measuring

Finding Optimal Biological Dose
163
biological activities of the drug (e.g., inhibition of an oncogene pathway, gene
expression, or proliferation of T cells).
8.3.1
Utility-based risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Ä
U-BOIN uses utility to quantify the risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Ä, based on possible
outcomes that a patient can have. SpeciÔ¨Åcally, given binary yT and yE, there
are four possible outcomes for any patient in the trial: (yT , yE) = (0, 1) =
(no toxicity, eÔ¨Écacy); (0, 0) = (no toxicity, no eÔ¨Écacy); (1, 1) = (toxicity,
eÔ¨Écacy); and (1, 0) = (toxicity, no eÔ¨Écacy) (Table 8.1). The joint outcome
(no toxicity, eÔ¨Écacy) is the most desirable, whereas (toxicity, no eÔ¨Écacy) is the
least desirable, and the other two are in between. We quantify the desirability
of each outcome by assigning it a utility (score), which should be elicited
from physicians to reÔ¨Çect the risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Äunderlying their medical
decisions.
One way to elicit the utility is given as follows: Ô¨Åx the score of the most
desirable outcome (no toxicity, eÔ¨Écacy) as œÖ01 = 100 and the score of the least
desirable outcome (toxicity, no eÔ¨Écacy) as œÖ10 = 0, and then ask clinicians
to use them as a reference to specify scores œÖ00 and œÖ11 for the other two
outcomes, i.e., (no toxicity, no eÔ¨Écacy) and (toxicity, eÔ¨Écacy), respectively to
quantify the risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Äunder each outcome. œÖ00 and œÖ11 should be
non-negative and ‚àà[0, 100]. Table 8.1 lists an example of an elicited utility
table. The speciÔ¨Åcation of œÖ00 and œÖ11 needs to reÔ¨Çect the clinical desirability
of the corresponding outcomes: setting œÖ11 ‚â•œÖ00 means that having a re-
sponse is more important than not having a toxicity, and patients are willing
to tolerate toxicity in exchange for eÔ¨Écacy; otherwise, œÖ11 < œÖ00 should be
used. From the dose-Ô¨Ånding perspective, setting œÖ11 ‚â•œÖ00 is usually more
meaningful and leads to a more thorough exploration of the dose space than
setting œÖ11 < œÖ00.
Let (p01, p00, p11, p10) denote the probabilities of observing the four pos-
sible toxicity‚ÄìeÔ¨Écacy outcomes at a certain dose d. The probability vector
(p01, p00, p11, p10) typically varies across doses, i.e., as a function of d. For ease
of exposition, we suppress the argument d. Averaging over the four possible
outcomes, the desirability (or mean utility) of dose d is
u = p01œÖ01 + p00œÖ00 + p11œÖ11 + p10œÖ10.
(8.2)

A higher value of u indicates a higher dose desirability in terms of the risk-
beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Ä.
Revisit the example in Section 8.1 and assume the utility in Table
8.1, when the true toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy probabilities are (œÄT 1, . . . , œÄT 5) =
(0.05, 0.12, 0.27, 0.35, 0.50) and (œÄE1, . . . , œÄE5) = (0.20, 0.35, 0.36, 0.37, 0.38),
respectively, the desirability of d3 is u3 = 50.8, and the desirability of d2
is u2 = 56.2. By using the utility approach, we naturally account for the
risk‚ÄìbeneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Äand correctly identify that d2 is more desirable than d3.
Similarly, if (œÄE1, . . . , œÄE5) = (0.01, 0.05, 0.30, 0.60, 0.60), the desirability of d4
is u4 = 62.0, which is higher than u3 = 47.2 of dose d3.
In our experience, clinicians can quickly understand what the utility means
and provide utility scores, since the framework resembles clinical practice and
the utility scores reÔ¨Çect actual clinical judgment. After completing this pro-
cess, simulation should be performed to verify the operating characteristics of
the design. In some cases, the simulation results may motivate slight modiÔ¨Å-
cation of some of the numerical utility values.
One possible criticism for using the utility values is that they require sub-
jective input. However, we are inclined to view this as a strength rather than
a weakness. The process of specifying the utility requires clinicians to care-
fully consider the potential risks and beneÔ¨Åts of the treatment that underlie
their clinical decision making in a more formal way and incorporate that into
the trial. In addition, our simulation study and previous studies (Guo and
Yuan, 2017; Liu et al., 2018; Murray et al., 2018; Zhou et al., 2019; Lin et al.,
2020b) show that the design is generally not sensitive to small diÔ¨Äerences in
the numerical values of the utility as long as the values reÔ¨Çect a similar trend.
Compared to the toxicity-eÔ¨Écacy trade-oÔ¨Äcontour approach used by the
EÔ¨ÄTox assign, the utility approach has several advantages. First, it is simpler
and only requires elicitation of the utility score of element outcomes that have
transparent clinical interpretation. In contrast, the process of constructing
the toxicity-eÔ¨Écacy trade-oÔ¨Äcontour is complicated. More problematically, it
assumes that the contour must follow a speciÔ¨Åc function form, equation (8.1),
which lacks clinical interpretation and justiÔ¨Åcation, and is likely to be false.
In our experience, it is often much easier for clinicians to understand and
quantify the risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Äbased on patient outcomes than based on
toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy probabilities.
Second, the utility approach is highly Ô¨Çexible and includes other risk-
beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Ämethods as special cases (Zhou et al., 2019; Lin et al., 2020b).
For example, by imposing the constraint œÖ00 + œÖ11 = 100, we have

Another important special case of the utility approach is to set œÖ00 = 0
and œÖ11 = 100 to turn oÔ¨Äthe toxicity-eÔ¨Écacy trade-oÔ¨Ä. Under this utility, we
favor the dose with the highest eÔ¨Écacy, which is appropriate for trials aiming
to identify the dose yielding the highest eÔ¨Écacy. The safety of the dose is
safeguarded by the admissible rules described below.
Third, the utility approach is highly scalable. It is directly applicable
to categorical yT and yE with more than two levels. Table 8.2 provides
an example of the utility approach for a trinary toxicity endpoint (mi-

(PD)/stable disease (SD)/partial response or complete response (PR/CR)).
In addition, Lin et al. (2020b) shows that it is similarly straightforward to
accommodate more than two endpoints. In contrast, it is not clear how to
apply the toxicity-eÔ¨Écacy trade-oÔ¨Äcontour approach to these cases.

8.3.3
Admissible rules
The U-BOIN design uses similar admissible rules as those of the EÔ¨ÄTox design.
Let œÜT and œÜE be the clinician-speciÔ¨Åed toxicity upper limit and eÔ¨Écacy lower
limit, respectively. Generally, œÜE can take the value of the target response rate
speciÔ¨Åed for a standard phase II trial. Because U-BOIN considers the toxicity‚Äì
eÔ¨Écacy trade-oÔ¨Ä, the value of œÜT should be set slightly higher (e.g, 0.05) than
the target toxicity rate used in conventional toxicity-based phase I designs. For
example, if 25% is an appropriate target toxicity rate that the conventional
phase I design used, then œÜT = 30% is a reasonable choice for U-BOIN.
Given the observed interim data D, a dose dj is deÔ¨Åned as acceptable (or
admissible) when it satisÔ¨Åes both of the following two criteria:
(Safety criterion)
Pr(œÄT j > œÜT | D) ‚â§cT ,
(8.7)
(EÔ¨Écacy criterion)
Pr(œÄEj < œÜE | D) ‚â§cE,
(8.8)

Finding Optimal Biological Dose
167
where cE and cT is a probability threshold. In general, we recommend
cT = 0.95 and cE = 0.90 as the default, which can be calibrated by simu-
lation. These cutoÔ¨Äs seem high, but actually are appropriate as their purpose
is to rule out excessively toxic and ineÔ¨Äective doses. Among admissible doses,
the dose assignment rule will allocate patients to the most desirable dose. In
other words, even if the admissible dose set includes some doses that are not
particularly safe or eÔ¨Écacious, the design likely will not assign patients to
these suboptimal doses. Due to the large uncertainty of small sample sizes,
using small values for cT and cE will inadvertently eliminate the doses that are
actually admissible and thus aÔ¨Äect the operating characteristics of the design.
Evaluation of the admissibility of a dose is straightforward under the
U-BOIN design because f(œÄT j | D) and f(œÄEj | D) follow beta distribu-
tions given by (8.6). In contrast, evaluation of the admissibility of a dose is
much more complicated in the EÔ¨ÄTox design and requires Ô¨Åtting the com-
plex Gumbel-Morgenstern copula model using a Markov chain Monte Carlo
method. Let A(D) denote the set of admissible doses. During the trial, only
doses in A(D) can be used to treat patients.
8.3.4
Dose-Ô¨Ånding algorithm
The U-BOIN design consists of two seamless, connected stages (Figure 8.3).
The objective of Stage I is to quickly explore the dose space to identify a set
of admissible doses that are reasonably eÔ¨Écacious and safe for Stage II. The
objective of Stage II is to optimize the dose based on the utility. In Stage
I, we conduct dose escalation based on the BOIN design using only yT , but
yE is also collected and will be used for decision making in Stage II. Given
the exploratory nature of Stage I, if yT has more than two categories, we
dichotomize it as DLT/no-DLT to facilitate the exploration of the dose space.
This is in line with clinical practice and serves well for the purpose of Stage I.
Consider a phase I-II trial with J doses, d1 < ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ < dJ, let ÀÜœÄT j = nT j/nj
be the observed toxicity rate of dose dj. Based on the BOIN‚Äôs dose escalation
and de-escalation boundaries (Œªe, Œªd), the dose-Ô¨Ånding algorithm of U-BOIN
in Stage I proceeds as follows:
A1
Patients in the Ô¨Årst cohort are treated at the lowest dose, or a
physician-speciÔ¨Åed dose.
A2

cohort of patients:

‚Ä¢ If ÀÜœÄT j > Œªd, de-escalate the dose to level j ‚àí1.
‚Ä¢ Otherwise, i.e., Œªe < ÀÜœÄT j ‚â§Œªd, stay at the current dose level
j.

A3
Repeat Step A2 until the number of patients treated on one of the
doses reaches s1, and then move to Stage II. Zhou et al. (2019)
recommended s1 = 12 as the default value, while s1 = 9 to 15
generally yields good operating characteristics.
Stage II proceeds as follows:
B1
Let j‚àódenote the highest dose level that has been tried. If ÀÜœÄT j‚àó‚â§Œªe
and j‚àóis not the highest dose in the trial, escalate the dose to level
j‚àó+ 1 for treating the next cohort of patients; otherwise, proceed
to Step B2.

Finding Optimal Biological Dose
169
B2
Update the admissible dose set A(D) based on the interim data D.
If no dose is admissible, terminate the trial and no dose should be
selected as OBD. Otherwise, use one of the following strategies to
allocate the next cohort of patients:
(i) Equally randomize the next cohort of patients to a dose in
A(D).
(ii) Assign the next cohort of patients to the admissible dose that
has the largest posterior mean utility ÀÜu.
(iii) Adaptively randomize the next cohort of patients to a dose in
A(D) with a probability proportional to its ÀÜu.
B3
Repeat Steps B1 and B2 until the maximum sample size N is ex-
hausted or the number of patients treated at one of the doses reaches
s2 (> s1), and then select the OBD as the admissible dose (i.e.,
‚ààA(D)) that has the largest posterior mean utility.
In Stage I, following the BOIN design, we impose an overdose control rule as
follows: if Pr(œÄT j > œÜT | Dj) > 0.95 and nj ‚â•3, dose level j and higher are
eliminated from the trial, and the trial is terminated if the lowest dose level
is eliminated, where Pr(œÄT j > œÜT | Dj) > 0.95 is evaluated based a beta-
binomial model with the uniform prior. Once the trial moves on to Stage II,
this overdose control rule is seamlessly merged as the safety criterion of the
inadmissible rule deÔ¨Åned in Section 8.3.3.
For Stage II Step B1, the reason that we perform dose escalation when
ÀÜœÄT j‚àó‚â§Œªe is to allow the trial to continue exploring the dose space, given
that the highest tried dose is safe, to reduce the risk of being stuck at a local
suboptimal dose due to large variation caused by small sample size.
In Stage II Step B2, the three strategies generally yield similar performance
in identifying the OBD, but have a diÔ¨Äerent emphasis on patient allocation.
Determining which one to use should be based on speciÔ¨Åc trial considerations.
Strategy (i) (i.e., equal randomization) is the easiest to implement and al-
lows more uniform learning of admissible doses. In addition, equal randomiza-
tion does not require eÔ¨Écacy readout, avoiding the common logistic diÔ¨Éculty
that the eÔ¨Écacy endpoint may take a relatively long time to be ascertained.
Also, equal randomization is useful to balance confounders across admissible
doses to facilitate reliable identiÔ¨Åcation of OBD particularly when patients
are highly heterogenous (e.g., all-comer trials). Equal randomization can be
modiÔ¨Åed to a Ô¨Åxed-ratio randomization. For example, based on the number of
patients allocated at Stage I, we choose a Ô¨Åxed randomization ratio such that
at the end of Stage II, the sample size at each admissible dose is expected to
be the same.
In comparison, Strategy (ii), i.e., pick the winner, aims to maximize patient
beneÔ¨Åt by assigning the next cohort to the currently estimated optimal dose.
This approach tends to assign more patients to OBD than Strategy (i). As
a trade-oÔ¨Ä, in certain scenarios it may have a slightly lower probability of

170
Model-Assisted Bayesian Designs for Dose Finding and Optimization
selecting OBD as it may be stuck at local suboptimal doses, especially when
the sample size is small. This issue, however, is generally minor. Due the small
sample size, the estimated optimal dose often associates high uncertainty, and
varies when data accumulate. Thus, Strategy (ii) actually behaves rather like
adaptive randomization, i.e., Strategy (iii), although the latter exibits slightly
more randomness by design. Compared to Strategy (iii), Strategy (ii) has
similar performance, but is easier to understand and implement as described
below. Thus, we generally recommend Strategy (i) or (ii). See Zhou et al.
(2019) for more details and the numerical comparison of the three patient
allocation strategies.
Compared to the EÔ¨ÄTox design, one prominent advantage of U-BOIN is
that its dose assignment rules can be pretabulated in decision tables and
included in the trial protocol before the trial starts. To conduct the trial, no
complicated calculation is needed. The investigator can simply use the decision
tables to determine dose assignment. SpeciÔ¨Åcally, Stage I and Stage II Step
B1 are guided by the BOIN design decision table (see Section 3.4 for details),
and Steps B2 and B3 can be implemented based on the pre-calculated utility
table. Table 8.3 provides the utility table when the number of patients at a
dose is three or six, with the elicited utility scores in Table 8.1, œÜT = 0.3,
œÜE = 0.3, cT = 0.95, and cE = 0.9. A similar utility table can be generated
for any given n using the U-BOIN software described later. To illustrate the
use of the table, suppose at an interim of the trial, at d1, three patients were
treated with no toxicity and one eÔ¨Écacy, and at d2, six patients were treated
with two toxicity and two eÔ¨Écacy. By looking up the table, the estimated
utility of d1 is 57.5 and the estimated utility of d2 is 47.1. Thus, if we use
Strategy (ii) to allocate patients, the next cohort should be treated at dose
level one as it has higher desirability.
Of note, in Table 8.3, ÀÜu depends only on nT and nE because the utility
score used (Table 8.1) satisÔ¨Åes œÖ00 + œÖ11 = 100. In this case, as described
previously, u is a function of œÄT and œÄE, see equation (8.3), thus ÀÜu depends
only on nT and nE. In the general case that œÖ00 + œÖ11 Ã∏= 100, ÀÜu is a function
of y10, y00, y01, and y11. The utility table still can be constructed, but with
more entries because of more possible data patterns given n.

U-BOIN‚Äôs two-stage approach, which Ô¨Årst performs toxicity-based dose es-

eÔ¨Écacy. As a result, U-BOIN and BOIN12 demonstrate diÔ¨Äerent design char-
acteristics and are suitable for diÔ¨Äerent trial objectives.
U-BOIN is more appropriate for the case where both identiÔ¨Åcation of OBD
and evaluation of the dose-toxicity proÔ¨Åle of the drug are of interest, whereas
BOIN12 is a better choice when the primary goal is to identify OBD. For
the purpose of Ô¨Ånding the OBD, BOIN12 is more eÔ¨Écient and often requires
a smaller sample size than U-BOIN. For example, for drugs with a low, Ô¨Çat
dose-toxicity curve and an increase-then-plateau dose-eÔ¨Écacy curve, U-BOIN
will Ô¨Årst escalate all the way up to the highest dose, and then turn around

Finding Optimal Biological Dose
173
to search for OBD. In contrast, BOIN12 will quickly reach and stay at OBD
because it considers both eÔ¨Écacy and toxicity from the beginning of the trial.
Compared to BOIN12, U-BOIN has some operational advantages. BOIN12
requires that the eÔ¨Écacy endpoint must be scored quickly enough so that the
dose assignment decision is timely for each new cohort. U-BOIN is lenient
on this requirement as its Ô¨Årst stage often ‚Äúbuys‚Äù suÔ¨Écient time to evaluate
the eÔ¨Écacy endpoint, especially when the equal or Ô¨Åxed-ratio randomization
is employed in Stage II of U-BOIN. Section 8.5 introduces an extension of
BOIN12 that is able to handle delayed eÔ¨Écacy or/and toxicity endpoints.
8.4.1
Utility estimation using quasi-binomial likelihood
Similar to U-BOIN, BOIN12 uses utility to quantify the risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-
oÔ¨Äand desirability of each dose. As described in Section 8.3.1, the utility
approach has the advantage of being easy to understand and implement, and
it is highly Ô¨Çexible and scalable to accommodate various types of endpoints.
We adopt the same notation as the previous section and focus on a binary
toxicity endpoint yT and binary eÔ¨Écacy endpoint yE.
Let (p01, p00, p11, p10) denote the probabilities of observing the four possi-
ble values of (yT , yE), i.e., (0, 1), (0, 0), (1, 1), and (1, 0), at a certain dose d.
For notational brevity, we suppress that (p01, p00, p11, p10) is a function of d.
Let (œÖ01, œÖ00, œÖ11, œÖ10) denote the utility scores, which are elicited from clin-
icians to quantify the desirability of the four possible outcomes of (yT , yE),
see Table 8.1 for an example. Similar to Section 8.3.1, we set the utility of the
most desirable outcome œÖ01 = 100 to Ô¨Åx the scale of the score. The desirability
(or mean utility) of dose d is given by
u = p01œÖ01 + p00œÖ00 + p11œÖ11 + p10œÖ10.
(8.9)
A higher value of u indicates a higher dose desirability in terms of the risk-
beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Ä.
The BOIN12 design makes decisions of dose escalation/de-escalation based
on f(u|D), the posterior distribution of u given the interim data D. A straight-
forward approach is to derive the posterior f(u | D) based on the deÔ¨Ånition
(8.9) and the Dirichlet-multinomial model, described in Section 8.3.2.
Lin et al. (2020b) proposed a more straightforward approach by di-
rectly modeling u based on the quasi-binomial likelihood theory (Papke and
Wooldridge, 1996; Yuan et al., 2007). DeÔ¨Åne the standardized desirability
Àúu = u/100. Because Àúu ‚àà[0, 1] and takes a form of the weighted average of
(p01, p00, p11, p10), it can be viewed as a probability and modeled using the
binomial distribution with ‚Äúquasi-binomial‚Äù data (x, n), where
x = 100y01 + œÖ00y00 + œÖ11y11
100
.
(8.10)

174
Model-Assisted Bayesian Designs for Dose Finding and Optimization
This quasi-number x can be interpreted as the number of ‚Äúevents‚Äù ob-
served from n patients treated at dose d, given the event probability Àúu. This
interpretation follows because E(x) = n √ó Àúu. Unlike the standard binomial
data, x is a real value that can take any value between 0 and n instead of
integers only, and thus it is known as quasi-binomial. When œÖ00 + œÖ11 = 100,
the expression of x can be further simpliÔ¨Åed to
x = œÖ11nE + œÖ00(n ‚àínT )
100
.
The quasi-binomial likelihood of the unknown Àúu based on the interim data D

(8.11)
By default, the non-informative uniform prior distribution with Œ±u = Œ≤u = 1
is used. A similar quasi-binomial approach was used by Yuan et al. (2007) to
model the normalized equivalent toxicity score that maps toxicity grades into
a fraction of dose limiting toxicity under the parametric CRM model.
8.4.2
Admissible rules and dose comparison
BOIN12 uses the same admissible rules as those of U-BOIN, described in Sec-
tion 8.3.3. BrieÔ¨Çy, let œÜT and œÜE denote the toxicity upper limit and eÔ¨Écacy
lower limit, respectively, speciÔ¨Åed by clinicians. Generally, œÜE may take the
value of the target response rate speciÔ¨Åed for a standard phase II trial. The
value of œÜT should be set slightly higher (e.g., 0.05) than the target toxicity
rate used in conventional toxicity-based phase I designs to provide additional
space for toxicity‚ÄìeÔ¨Écacy trade-oÔ¨Ä. For example, if 30% is an appropriate tar-
get toxicity rate that the conventional phase I design used, then œÜT = 35% is
a reasonable choice for BOIN12.
Given the interim data D, a dose dj is deÔ¨Åned as acceptable (or admissi-
ble) when it satisÔ¨Åes both of the following two criteria:
(Safety criterion)
Pr(œÄT j > œÜT | D) ‚â§cT ,
(EÔ¨Écacy criterion)
Pr(œÄEj < œÜE | D) ‚â§cE,
where cE and cT are probability thresholds. These two posterior quantities
can be evaluated based on the posterior of œÄT and œÄE, given by equation
(8.6). Denote the set of admissible doses by A(D), which should be updated
with interim data D. Only doses in A(D) can be used to treat patients. In
general, we recommend cT = 0.95 and cE = 0.90 as the default, which can be

Finding Optimal Biological Dose
175
further calibrated by simulation, see Section 8.3.3 for more discussion on the
choice of cT and cE.
In BOIN12, the posterior probability PPj = Pr(uj > ub | Dj) is used to
determine the most desirable dose within A(D) for treating a new cohort of
patients, where Dj is the data observed at dj. Here, ub is a prespeciÔ¨Åed utility
benchmark used to evaluate dose desirability. Between two admissible doses
dj and dj‚Ä≤,
‚Ä¢ If PPj > PPj‚Ä≤, then dj is more desirable than dj‚Ä≤.
‚Ä¢ If PPj < PPj‚Ä≤, then dj is less desirable than dj‚Ä≤.
‚Ä¢ If PPj = PPj‚Ä≤, then dj and dj‚Ä≤ are equally desirable.
In principle, the third case occurs if and only if Dj = Dj‚Ä≤. The posterior prob-
ability PPj automatically accounts for the uncertainty in estimating u, leading
to more reasonable decision making of dose escalation and de-escalation. In
contrast, most existing designs use the point estimate ÀÜu = x/n to choose the
optimal dose, which ignores the uncertainty of the estimate. To see the im-
portance of accounting for estimation uncertainty, suppose dose dj has been
used to treat two patients and ÀÜuj = 60, and dose dj‚Ä≤ has been used to treat 10
patients and ÀÜuj‚Ä≤ = 59. Although ÀÜuj > ÀÜuj‚Ä≤, because ÀÜuj‚Ä≤ is much more reliable, a
better decision actually is to choose dj‚Ä≤ for treating the next cohort of patients
as stipulated by the posterior probability approach.
Evaluating PPj requires specifying a benchmark ub for comparison. The
value of ub can be elicited by clinicians to reÔ¨Çect their expectation. Lin et al.
(2020b) recommended the following default value of ub that yields desirable
operating characteristics in a variety of scenarios. Given œÜT and œÜE and as-
suming independence between toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy, the highest utility that is
deemed undesirable is given by
u = 100œÜE(1 ‚àíœÜT ) + œÖ00(1 ‚àíœÜT )(1 ‚àíœÜE) + œÖ11œÜT œÜE.
The recommended default value of ub is
ub = (100 + u)/2,
representing that a utility value lies in the middle between u and the maximum
utility 100. Alternatively, one can also use the weighted average as the choice
for ub, i.e., ub = œâ100 + (1 ‚àíœâ)u, where œâ ‚àà[0, 1] is a non-negative weight.
In general, the weight œâ controls the aggressiveness of the trial: the larger the
value of œâ, the more aggressive the dose exploration.
One prominent feature of BOIN12 is that the desirability can be pre-
calculated and included in the trial protocol, due to the use of the quasi-
binomial likelihood. SpeciÔ¨Åcally, given the maximum sample size N, all
possible outcome combinations (n, nT , nE) (or (y01, y00, y11, y10)) can be enu-
merated, and the corresponding posterior probabilities PPj can be computed
based on (8.11). By sorting all the possible values of PPj from the smallest
to the largest, we can assign the ordered value from 0, 1, . . . to each possible

176
Model-Assisted Bayesian Designs for Dose Finding and Optimization
outcome combination. These ordered values are called rank-based desirability
scores (RDS). RDS allows for a simpler presentation of the desirability of a
dose using integers. Comparing the posterior probabilities PPj between two
doses is equivalent to comparing their corresponding RDS; therefore, RDS
can be used as a convenient way to identify a more desirable dose. For ex-
ample, based on the utility in Table 8.1, Table 8.5 lists RDS with a cohort
size of three to nine patients treated at a dose, with the highest acceptable
toxicity probability œÜT = 0.35 and the lowest acceptable eÔ¨Écacy probability
œÜE = 0.25.
During the trial, the desirability of a dose can be simply determined as
follows: count the number of patients treated at that dose n, the number
of patients who experienced toxicity nT , and the number of patients who
experienced eÔ¨Écacy nE (and the number of patients who experienced eÔ¨Écacy
without toxicity when œÖ00 + œÖ11 Ã∏= 100), then look up Table 8.5 to determine
its RDS.
For example, suppose at a certain point of the trial, the numbers of pa-
tients treated at the Ô¨Årst three doses are three, six, and three; the numbers
of toxicities are zero, one, and two; and the numbers of eÔ¨Écacy outcomes are
zero, three, and one. By looking up Table 8.5, the RDS of the Ô¨Årst three doses
are 35, 56, and 31, respectively. Because dose d2 has the highest RDS, it is
more desirable than d1 and d3. Table 8.5 assumes a cohort size of three, but
the BOIN12 design is Ô¨Çexible and can allow any prespeciÔ¨Åed cohort size. The
RDS table can be easily generated using the BOIN12 companion software.
It is worth noting the diÔ¨Äerence between BOIN12 and U-BOIN. U-BOIN
uses the posterior mean of u (i.e., ÀÜu) to determine the most desirable dose
within A(D) for treating a new cohort of patients. The utility table of U-
BOIN (e.g., Table 8.3) lists the value of ÀÜu for each possible interim data, not
the rank of ÀÜu. Nevertheless, in principle, for U-BOIN, a RDS table similar to
that of BOIN12 can also be developed by replacing the value of ÀÜu with its
rank among all possible values.
8.4.3

rule of the BOIN12 design is illustrated in Figure 8.4 and described as follows:
1.
Patients in the Ô¨Årst cohort are treated at the lowest dose d1, or the
physician-speciÔ¨Åed dose.
2.
Based on the interim data D, we obtain RDS for each dose using
the RDS table (e.g., Table 8.5). Suppose j is the current dose level.
To assign a dose to the next cohort of patients,

3.

179
Step 2(a) says that if the observed toxicity rate is high (i.e., ÀÜœÄT j > Œªd),
we should de-escalate the dose. Step 2(b) says that if there are suÔ¨Écient data
(nj ‚â•N ‚àó) to support that the toxicity probability of the current dose dj
is moderate (i.e., Œªe < ÀÜœÄT j ‚â§Œªd), escalating the dose to dj+1 may cause
overdosing. We then examine whether the adjacent lower dose dj‚àí1 provides
a better treatment beneÔ¨Åt (i.e., desirability) than the current dose dj. If so,
we treat the next cohort of patients at dose level j ‚àí1; otherwise stay at
the current level j. Rule 2(c) says that if the observed toxicity rate at the
current dose dj is low (ÀÜœÄT j ‚â§Œªe) or moderate ( Œªe < ÀÜœÄT j ‚â§Œªd) but with high
uncertainty (n < N ‚àó), then we examine whether the adjacent higher dose dj+1
or the lower dose dj‚àí1 can provide a better treatment beneÔ¨Åt (i.e., desirability)
than the current dose dj. The next cohort of patients will be treated at the
dose with the highest desirability among the adjacent doses. In Step 2(b), the
higher dose dj+1 is not considered due to the fact that, given that there is
substantial evidence (i.e., nj ‚â•N ‚àó) that dj is already close to the de-escalation
boundary Œªd and dj+1 is likely to be overly toxic. Therefore, N ‚àóis a cutoÔ¨Ä
for suÔ¨Éciency of the data, and a larger value of N ‚àóencourages more active
exploration of new doses. By default, Lin et al. (2020b) recommended N ‚àó= 6.
When needed, N ‚àócan be further calibrated by simulation to obtain certain
design properties, e.g., using a larger N ‚àóencourages faster dose exploration.
During the trial, only admissible doses can be given to incoming patients,
and doses that are not admissible should be eliminated. In other words, the
decision rule in Step 2 should be applied only to the doses in A(D). For
example, in Step 2(c), if only dj‚àí1, dj ‚ààA(D) and dj+1 /‚ààA(D), then when
we apply the rule 2(c), we should choose the level from {j ‚àí1, j} that has the
highest RDS to treat the next cohort of patients. At any time, if all doses are
eliminated, the trial should be stopped with no dose selected as OBD.
For ethical considerations, BOIN12 always assigns the next cohort of pa-
tients to the dose with the highest estimate of desirability. With a small sample
size, this myopic approach may cause the dose Ô¨Ånding to get stuck at a locally
optimal dose. To alleviate that issue, a dose exploration rule can be imposed:
treat the next cohort of patients at the next higher dose if the following three
conditions are all satisÔ¨Åed:
‚Ä¢ The number of patients treated at the current dose dj is greater than eight.
‚Ä¢ The observed toxicity rate ÀÜœÄT j is less than the de-escalation boundary Œªd.
‚Ä¢ The next higher dose has never been used for treating patients.
When the trial completes, the Ô¨Ånal OBD is selected based on a two-step
procedure: in the Ô¨Årst step, we select MTD based on the target toxicity rate
œÜT so that any dose levels above the selected MTD are deemed overly toxic; in
the second step, we then choose the dose level that has the highest desirability
among the doses that are not higher than MTD. SpeciÔ¨Åcally, at the end of the
trial, we Ô¨Årst obtain the observed marginal toxicity rates ÀÜœÄT j for each dose
level j = 1, . . . , J. To borrow information across dose levels, an isotonic re-
gression is performed on {ÀÜœÄT j} through the pool-adjacent-violators algorithm

8.4.4
Operating characteristics
Lin et al. (2020b) carried out a simulation study to compare the operating
characteristics of BOIN12, the model-based EÔ¨ÄTox design, the toxicity and ef-
Ô¨Åcacy probability interval (TEPI) design (Li et al., 2017), and the conventional
design consisting of a dose escalation phase based on the 3+3 design followed
by a cohort expansion at the identiÔ¨Åed MTD (referred to as 3+3+CE). Five
scenarios with the marginal toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy rates of each of the Ô¨Åve dose
levels considered in the simulation study are provided in Table 8.6. The high-
est acceptable toxicity probability is œÜT = 0.35, and the lowest acceptable
eÔ¨Écacy probability is œÜE = 0.25. The utility in Table 8.1 is used to deÔ¨Åne
the toxicity‚ÄìeÔ¨Écacy trade-oÔ¨Ä. Patients are treated in cohorts of three, with a
maximum sample size of 36. The Simon‚Äôs two-stage design (Simon, 1989) is
used to monitor eÔ¨Écacy in the cohort expansion for 3+3+CE.
Figure 8.5 presents the simulation results based on 10,000 simulated trials
that quantify the operating characteristics of the designs, including the per-
centage of correct selection of OBD, the number of patients treated at OBD,
the number of patients treated at the overdoses (i.e., the doses with toxicity
rates > 0.35), and risk of poor allocation (i.e., the percentage of trials that
allocate < 36/5 = 7.2 patients to OBD). In general, BOIN12 has the best
overall performance among the four designs. It has the highest average per-
centage of correct selection of OBD and allocates the largest average number
of patients to OBD. In addition, BOIN12 is safer and more reliable than other
designs, as demonstrated by having amongst the smallest number of patients
treated at toxic doses and the lowest risk of poor allocation.
As expected, the 3 + 3 + CE design has the worst overall performance
because that design ignores the toxicity-eÔ¨Écacy trade-oÔ¨Äand does not target
OBD. The performance of the EÔ¨ÄTox design varies dramatically from one
scenario to another, depending on how well the assumed model reÔ¨Çects the true

8.5
TITE-BOIN12 design
One practical issue encountered when applying BOIN12, especially in trials of
targeted or immunotherapy agents, is that of possible late-onset eÔ¨Écacy and
toxicity. As a motivating example, consider a phase I/II renal cell carcinoma
trial. The objective is to determine OBD of a novel targeted agent, combined
with nivolumab at a Ô¨Åxed dose of 3 mg/kg daily for two weeks, in patients
with metastatic renal cell carcinoma. Toxicity is deÔ¨Åned as a grade 3 or 4 liver,
lung, gastrointestinal, or endocrine toxicity, or myelosuppression, within 45
days from the start of therapy according to Common Terminology Criteria for
Adverse Events (CTCAE) version 5. EÔ¨Écacy is deÔ¨Åned as achieving complete
response or partial response within 60 days. The expected accrual rate is three
patients per month. The challenge of designing this trial is that, on average,

184
Model-Assisted Bayesian Designs for Dose Finding and Optimization
six (or four) new patients will be enrolled by the time the previous cohort of
patients complete their eÔ¨Écacy (or toxicity) evaluation. The question is how
to treat these new patients in a timely fashion, given that the outcomes of
some of the previously treated patients are still pending.
Formally, let œÑT and œÑE denote the lengths of the assessment windows
for yT and yE, respectively. The value of œÑT and œÑE should be elicited from
clinicians and wide enough to capture all necessary toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy events
relevant to OBD determination. For example, œÑT may be the Ô¨Årst cycle of the
therapy (e.g., 21 or 28 days), while œÑE may be also the Ô¨Årst cycle of the therapy
(e.g., pharmacodynamic (PD) biomarkers), or several months after treatment
(e.g., tumor response). Let tT and tE denote the times to toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy,
respectively. For patients who will not experience toxicity or eÔ¨Écacy, deÔ¨Åne
tT = ‚àûand tE = ‚àû. The relation between (yT , yE) and (tT , tE) is given as
follows:
yT = 1{tT ‚â§œÑT },
and
yE = 1{tE ‚â§œÑE},
where 1{¬∑} denotes the indicator function, which takes a value of 1 if the event
speciÔ¨Åed in the braces occurs and zero otherwise.
Suppose that n patients have been accrued and treated at dose d, we
use the subscript i to indicate the data for the ith patient, i = 1, . . . , n.
The relationship between the multinomial variable (y01, y00, y11, y10) and the
individual patient-level data is
y01 = Pn
i=1 1 {(yT i, yEi) = (0, 1)} = Pn
i=1 1 {tT i > œÑT , tEi ‚â§œÑE} ,
y00 = Pn
i=1 1 {(yT i, yEi) = (0, 0)} = Pn
i=1 1 {tT i > œÑT , tEi > œÑE} ,
y11 = Pn
i=1 1 {(yT i, yEi) = (1, 1)} = Pn
i=1 1 {tT i ‚â§œÑT , tEi ‚â§œÑE} ,
y10 = Pn
i=1 1 {(yT i, yEi) = (1, 0)} = Pn
i=1 1 {tT i ‚â§œÑT , tEi > œÑE} .
Denote tU as the follow-up time. In cases of late-onset toxicity or eÔ¨Écacy, the
issue is that the time to toxicity or eÔ¨Écacy may not be observed as fast as
the patients‚Äô interarrival time. As a result, tU may censor tT or tE or both
of them. In other words, when tU is smaller than tT or tE, yT or yE may be
unobservable. Section 5.2 provides statistical properties of these missing data,
and why we cannot simply ignore them in decision making.
As an extension of BOIN12, the TITE-BOIN12 design addresses the late-
onset issue by using the follow-up times of pending patients to facilitate real-
time decision making to choose the optimal dose for new patients (Zhou et al.,
2022). More precisely, by treating the outcome data of the pending patients as
missing, two approaches, based on diÔ¨Äerent imputation techniques, have been
proposed: Bayesian data augmentation (BDA) (Little and Rubin, 2014) and
mean imputation based on the approximate likelihood (Lin and Yuan, 2020).
Bayesian data augmentation
BDA iterates between two steps: the imputation (I) step, in which the missing
yT and yE are imputed from their conditional posteriors, and the posterior

Finding Optimal Biological Dose
185
(P) step, in which the posterior samples of unknown parameters including
(p01, p00, p11, p10) and Àúu are simulated based on the imputed data. As the P
step is the same as BOIN12, we here focus on the I step, which involves the
conditional posteriors of missing yT and yE.
In the presence of late-onset toxicity or eÔ¨Écacy, there are three possible
missing patterns: (1) both yT and yE are missing, (2) only yT is missing, and
(3) only yE is missing. DeÔ¨Åne Œ¥T = 1{tU < œÑT ‚à©tU < tT } as the missing
indicator for toxicity, which takes a value of 1 if yT is missing. Similarly,
deÔ¨Åne Œ¥E = 1{tU < œÑE ‚à©tU < tE} as the missing indicator for eÔ¨Écacy. The
imputation steps for the three missing patterns are described as follows:
‚Ä¢ When both yT and yE are missing, we impute (yT , yE) by drawing a random
sample from the multinomial distribution with individual joint probabilities
given by
Pr {(yT , yE) = (a, b) | (Œ¥T , Œ¥E) = (1, 1)} =
pabSab
P1
a‚Ä≤=0

respective assessment windows. As a result, wT = tU/œÑT , and wE = tU/œÑE.
This assumption seems strong, but it is very robust for the purpose of dose

Mean imputation based on the approximated likelihood
One limitation of BDA is that it is computationally intensive. A second ap-
proach is to use mean imputation to deal with the missing data through the use
of the approximated likelihood approach by Lin and Yuan (2020). The key ob-
servation is that, in the presence of pending outcomes, the reason that BOIN12
cannot be directly applied is that the quasi-number of events x given by equa-
tion (8.10) cannot be calculated. Zhou et al. (2022) proposed to approximate x,
and thus the quasi-binomial likelihood, by replacing the pending/missing val-
ues of yT and yE with their expectations E(yT | Œ¥T = 1) = Pr(yT = 1 | Œ¥T = 1)
and E(yE | Œ¥E = 1) = Pr(yE = 1 | Œ¥E = 1), respectively.
Depending on the values of Œ¥T and Œ¥E, patients can be divided into four
types, i.e., (Œ¥T , Œ¥E) = (0, 0), (1, 0), (0, 1), (1, 1). The quasi-number of events x
can be approximated as follows,

In this equation, the Ô¨Årst term corresponds to those patients whose yT and
yE are both observed (i.e., (Œ¥T , Œ¥E) = (0, 0)), thus their contribution to the
quasi-number of events x can be directly computed based on the observed
data. The last three terms correspond to the patients with at least one of yT
and yE pending, i.e., Œ¥T + Œ¥E > 0, and involve the mean imputation of yT
and yE respectively by Pr(yT = a | Œ¥T = 1) and Pr(yE = 1 | Œ¥E = b), a, b =
0, 1, based on the assumption that yT and yE are ‚Äúworking‚Äù independent.
Technically speaking, the correlation between eÔ¨Écacy and toxicity can be
modeled. The small sample sizes of phase I/II trials, however, provide limited
information to estimate the correlation parameter reliably. The independence
assumption for pending outcomes seems strong, but it makes the method
simple and computationally fast. Zhou et al. (2022) showed by numerical
studies that the TITE-BOIN12 design is remarkably robust to the violation
of this assumption. This may be because the assumption is only used for the
patients who have pending data. For patients with both yT and yE observed,
the independence assumption is not made. When the trial progresses, the
percentage of observed data increases, limiting the impact of the violation of
the independence assumption.
By assuming that given yq = 1, the time-to-event outcome tq is a uniform
random variable over (0, œÑq), we have Pr(Œ¥q = 1 | yq = 1) = Pr(tq > tU | yq =

The dose-Ô¨Ånding algorithm for TITE-BOIN12, using either BDA or ap-
proximated likelihood methods, is the same as that for BOIN12. At each
interim decision, TITE-BOIN12 needs to update the admissible set A(D), the
estimate for the marginal toxicity probability ÀÜœÄT at the current dose, and the
desirability PPj for each dose considered in the trial. In addition, to avoid
risky decisions caused by sparse data, TITE-BOIN12 imposes the following
accrual suspension rule: if more than 50% of the patients have pending DLT

188
Model-Assisted Bayesian Designs for Dose Finding and Optimization
or eÔ¨Écacy outcomes at the current dose, suspend the accrual to wait for more
data to become available.
Of note, another appealing feature of TITE-BOIN12 is that it naturally
accommodates the case that some patients may be evaluable for toxicity, but
not evaluable for eÔ¨Écacy (e.g., patients are oÔ¨Ätreatment due to toxicity).
These patients can be regarded as yT are observed and yE are (permanently)
pending, and thus directly incorporated into the utility estimation and deci-
sion making.
8.6
Other model-assisted phase I/II designs
8.6.1
uTPI design
Similar to the BOIN12 design, the uTPI design (Shi et al., 2021) also adopts
the Dirichlet-multinomial and quasi-binomial approaches to model the joint
eÔ¨Écacy‚Äìtoxicity and quasi-binomial utility outcomes, respectively. Unlike
BOIN12, whose decisions are made based on the point estimate of the toxicity
rate and the posterior probability distribution of the utility, the uTPI design
is a seamless extension of the keyboard design (a.k.a., the m-TPI2 design),
and it uses the toxicity and utility intervals in determining the optimal dose
for the incoming patients.
SpeciÔ¨Åcally, following the idea of the keyboard design, the uTPI design
starts by partitioning the support of the probability of toxicity (0, 1) into a
series of intervals of equal width œµ, which results in KT toxicity intervals,
IT,k, k = 1, . . . , KT . Similarly, the utility support (0, 100) also can be par-
titioned into KU intervals, IU,k, k = 1, . . . , KU, based on an equal width Œ¥.
The parameter œµ (or Œ¥) denotes the indiÔ¨Äerence margin for toxicity (or util-
ity), which means that any two dose levels whose toxicity probabilities (or
mean utilities) lying within the same toxicity (or utility) interval are treated
indiÔ¨Äerently in terms of toxicity or mean utility. The reason for specifying the
indiÔ¨Äerence intervals is that the sample size in early-phase dose-Ô¨Ånding trials
is typically small, and thus it is diÔ¨Écult to diÔ¨Äerentiate two dose levels that
have similar toxicity probabilities or mean utilities.
For illustration, we take œµ = 0.1 and Œ¥ = 10, leading to 10 toxic-
ity intervals IT,1 = (0, 0.1), . . . , IT,10 = (0.9, 1) and 10 utility intervals
IU,1 = (0, 10), . . . , IT,10 = (90, 100). By combining the toxicity and utility
intervals, the equal-width ‚Äúkeys‚Äù in a one-dimensional keyboard design are
extended to equal-area ‚Äúsquares‚Äù on a two-dimensional chessboard, as shown
in Figure 8.6.
Mimicking the keyboard design, the strongest toxicity interval that pos-
sesses the largest posterior probability can be identiÔ¨Åed based on data D(d)

During the trial conduct, uTPI only treats the patients using doses in
the admissible set A(D) as deÔ¨Åned in BOIN12 and U-BOIN. If no dose is
admissible, the trial should be terminated early.
Similar to BOIN12 and U-BOIN designs, the uTPI design also has a concise
decision structure such that a dose-assignment decision table can be calculated
before the trial starts and can be used throughout the trial, which simpliÔ¨Åes
its practical implementation. More details about the implementation of the
uTPI design can be found in Shi et al. (2021).
8.6.2
STEIN and BOIN-ET designs
In some trials, OBD is deÔ¨Åned as the lowest safe dose that has the maximum
eÔ¨Écacy, instead of the safe dose that has the highest utility. In general, the
designs described previously can be readily adapted to these trials simply by
properly specifying the utility functions, e.g., setting œÖ11 = 100 and œÖ00 = 0 in
the BOIN12, U-BOIN, and uTPI designs. Without using utility, some model-
assisted designs have been proposed to directly Ô¨Ånd the OBD that has the
maximum eÔ¨Écacy.
Lin and Yin (2017b) proposed the STEIN design on the basis of the stan-
dard BOIN design. Besides using BOIN‚Äôs toxicity-based rule to determine
whether a dose is safe or not, the STEIN design additionally uses the hy-
potheses stated next to determine whether the eÔ¨Écacy at a dose is acceptable
or not. SpeciÔ¨Åcally, at each dose dj, two point hypotheses are deÔ¨Åned:
HE
0j : œÄE(dj) = œà1
versus
HE
1j : œÄE(dj) = œà2,

where œà1 denotes a clinically uninteresting response rate, and œà2 denotes a
clinically desired response rate. Let ÀÜœÄE(dj) = nE(dj)/n(dj) be the observed
eÔ¨Écacy rate at dose dj. The null hypothesis HE
0j is rejected and dose dj is
considered to be eÔ¨Écacious if ÀÜœÄE(dj) ‚â•Œ¥, where Œ¥ is the boundary for the

and use expanded notations such as ÀÜœÄE(dj), nE(dj) and n(dj) to represent
the same things as ÀÜœÄEj, nEj and nj used previously.
Based on a similar procedure of BOIN, the optimal boundary of the ob-
served eÔ¨Écacy rate can be obtained,

By partitioning the two-dimensional eÔ¨Écacy‚Äìtoxicity space into several re-
gions (Figure 8.7), the dose-Ô¨Ånding rule of STEIN can be described as follows:
1.
Patients in the Ô¨Årst cohort are treated at the lowest dose d1, or the
physician-speciÔ¨Åed dose.
2.
Suppose j is the current dose level. To assign a dose to the next
cohort of patients:
(a) If ÀÜœÄT (dj) > Œªd, de-escalate the dose to level j ‚àí1.
(b) If ÀÜœÄT (dj) ‚â§Œªd and ÀÜœÄE(dj) ‚â•Œ¥, stay at the current dose.
(c) Otherwise, choose the dose that has the largest posterior prob-
ability of Pr (œÄE(d) > Œ¥ | D(d)), which can be obtained based
on the beta-binomial model (8.6).
3.
Repeat Step 2 until the maximum sample size N is reached.
To use the STEIN design in practice, Lin and Yin (2017b) recommended
setting œÜ1 = 0.75œÜ and œÜ2 = 1.25œÜ to obtain BOIN‚Äôs escalation/de-escalation
boundaries, and they found that œà1 ‚àà(0.2, 0.4) and œà2 ‚àà(0.6, 0.8) are suitable
for most practical scenarios. For example, when œÜ = 0.3, œà1 = 0.3, and œà2 =
0.8, the values of (Œªe, Œªd, Œ¥) are (0.26, 0.34, 0.56). When appropriate, these
design parameters can be further calibrated by simulation.
Similar to the STEIN design, the BOIN-ET design is another extension of
the BOIN design to identify OBD based on both eÔ¨Écacy and toxicity outcomes
(Takeda et al., 2018). Suppose the current dose level is j, according to Figure
8.8, the next dose assignment of BOIN-ET is as follows:
(1) If ÀÜœÄT (dj) ‚â§Œªe and ÀÜœÄE(dj) ‚â§Œ¥, then the current dose is safe but not eÔ¨Äective,
and the next dose should be escalated to level j + 1.
(2) If ÀÜœÄT (dj) > Œªd, then the current dose is too toxic and the next dose should
be de-escalated to level j ‚àí1.

(3) If ÀÜœÄT (dj) < Œªd and ÀÜœÄE(dj) > Œ¥, then the current dose is desirable in terms
of both eÔ¨Écacy and safety, then the next dose should not be changed.
(4) If Œªe < ÀÜœÄT (dj) ‚â§Œªd and ÀÜœÄE(dj) ‚â§Œ¥, then all possible decisions including
escalation, stay, and de-escalation are considered. Typically, set the admis-

following rules:
(a) If dose level j + 1 is untried, then the next dose is escalated to level
j + 1.
(b) Otherwise, the next dose is selected as the one with the maximum ob-
served eÔ¨Écacy rate. If there is a tie having the maximum observed eÔ¨É-
cacy rate, then the next dose is randomly chosen from the tied doses.
To determine the optimal values of (Œªe, Œªd, Œ¥), Takeda et al. (2018) con-
sidered the following six hypotheses at dose dj:
H1j : œÄT (dj) = œÜ1, œÄE(dj) = œà1,
H2j : œÄT (dj) = œÜ1, œÄE(dj) = œà2,
H3j : œÄT (dj) = œÜ, œÄE(dj) = œà1,
H4j : œÄT (dj) = œÜ, œÄE(dj) = œà2,
H5j : œÄT (dj) = œÜ2, œÄE(dj) = œà1,
H6j : œÄT (dj) = œÜ2, œÄE(dj) = œà2,
where the design parameters (œÜ, œÜ1, œÜ2, œà1, œà2) have similar interpretations
as in the STEIN design. By treating eÔ¨Écacy and toxicity jointly, the BOIN-
ET design Ô¨Ånds the optimal values that minimize the probability of incorrect
decisions using a numerical grid search. More details can be found in Takeda
et al. (2018).
To implement the BOIN-ET design, Takeda et al. (2018) recommended set-
ting œÜ1 = 0.1œÜ, œÜ2 = 1.4œÜ, œà1 = 0.6œà2, and assuming œÄ1j = ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ = œÄ6j = 1/6.
For illustration, consider the target toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy rates are (œÜ, œà2) =
(0.3, 0.6), the optimal values of (Œªe, Œªd, Œ¥) are calculated to be (0.14, 0.35, 0.48).
To accommodate potentially late-onset toxicity or eÔ¨Écacy, Takeda et al. (2020)
extended BOIN-ET to TITE-BOIN-ET along a similar line as TITE-BOIN12
using the approximated likelihood approach.
8.7
Software and case study
Software
The BOIN12, TITE-BOIN12, and U-BOIN designs can be implemented
using web applications provided at http://www.trialdesign.org. The
software allows users to generate the decision table, evaluate operat-
ing characteristics of the design, and generate the trial design template
for protocol preparation. The software for the EÔ¨ÄTox design is freely
available at the MD Anderson Cancer Center Software Download Web-
site https://biostatistics.mdanderson.org/softwaredownload/. The R

194
Model-Assisted Bayesian Designs for Dose Finding and Optimization
code for simulating the uTPI design is available at https://github.com/
ruitaolin/uTPI.
Case study
Platinum-Refractory Oral Cancer Trial
The objective of this phase I‚ÄìII
trial (Clinical Trials Registry-India identiÔ¨Åer: CTRI/2019/01/016837) is to
determine OBD for methotrexate when given along with erlotinib and cele-
coxib in treating patients with platinum-resistant or early-failure squamous
cell carcinoma of the oral cavity. DLT is scored based on the Common Termi-
nology Criteria for Adverse Events (version 4.03), and the eÔ¨Écacy endpoint
used in monitoring is the clinical beneÔ¨Åt rate at two months. Five doses of
methotrexate (i.e., 3, 6, 9, 12, and 15 mg/m2) are investigated in combination
with 150 mg erlotinib and 200 mg celecoxib.
Additional examples include a CD33 CAR T-cell therapy trial in pa-
tients with relapsed or refractory acute myeloid leukemia (ClinicalTrials.gov
IdentiÔ¨Åer: NCT04835519, based on BOIN12), and a trial that evaluates a
plant extract to treat breast cancer patients (ClinicalTrials.gov IdentiÔ¨Åer:
NCT05007444, based on U-BOIN).
We use the oral cancer trial as an example to illustrate the use of the
BOIN12 app to design phase I-II trials. The BOIN12 app can be selected and
launched from the BOIN Suite launchpad (Figure 8.9). The app includes six
FIGURE 8.9: The launchpad of web application ‚ÄúBOIN Suite‚Äù available at
http://www.trialdesign.org.

Finding Optimal Biological Dose
195
FIGURE 8.10: Specify doses and the sample size.
functional tabs and has the capability to implement the three main tasks: trial
design, trial conduct, and OBD determination. We design the trial using the
following three steps:
Step 1: Enter trial parameters
Doses and Sample Size
As shown in Figure 8.10, the number of doses un-
der investigation for methotrexate is Ô¨Åve, and the starting dose level is 1. The
total sample size is 36 and patients are treated in the cohort size of three,
which results in a total of 12 cohorts. We recommend the maximum sample
size N should be no less than 6 √ó J (i.e., the maximum sample size of the
3+3 design), where J is the number of doses. To reduce the sample size, the
‚Äúconvergence‚Äù stopping rule is implemented in BOIN12, that is, when m pa-
tients have been treated by the current dose and the decision is to stay, the
trial should be early stopped before the exhaustion of the maximum sample
size. This stopping criterion indicates that the trial can be stopped when the
dose Ô¨Ånding approximately converges to OBD. In this trial, m = 12 is used.
Because of this early stopping rule, the actual sample size used in the trial is
often smaller than N. The saving in sample size depends on the underlying
dose‚Äìtoxicity or dose‚ÄìeÔ¨Écacy scenario and can be evaluated through simula-
tion studies.

Model-Assisted Bayesian Designs for Dose Finding and Optimization
(a) Approach based on the utility score.
(b) Approach based on toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy probabilities.
FIGURE 8.11: Two approaches to specify the risk‚ÄìbeneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Äbased on
(a) utility scores for possible outcomes or (b) marginal toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy
probabilities.
Risk-beneÔ¨Åt Trade-oÔ¨ÄCriteria
In Figure 8.11 (a), the utility is used to
deÔ¨Åne the risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Ä(or desirability), where the outcome (toxic-
ity, eÔ¨Écacy) has a slightly higher utility score than (no toxicity, no eÔ¨Écacy).
Utility should be elicited from physicians to reÔ¨Çect the risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Ä
underlying their clinical decision making. After specifying the utility, simu-
lation should be performed to evaluate the operating characteristics of the
design. In some cases, the simulation results may motivate slight modiÔ¨Åcation
of some of the numerical utility values, although such modiÔ¨Åcation typically
has little or no eÔ¨Äect on the design‚Äôs operating characteristics. Given the

Finding Optimal Biological Dose
197
utility table, the desirability of a dose is characterized by the mean utility
(8.9), and a higher value of the mean utility means the dose is more desirable.
The software provides another option to specify the risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-
oÔ¨Äbased on the marginal toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy probabilities, see Figure 8.11
(b). As described in Section 8.3.1, this approach actually is a special case of
the utility approach with œÖ00 + œÖ11 = 100. For example, the utility speciÔ¨Åed
in Figure 8.11 (a) is equivalent to specifying the toxicity-eÔ¨Écacy trade-oÔ¨Äas
Pr(eÔ¨Écacy)-2/3Pr(toxicity). In other words, the risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨ÄspeciÔ¨Åed
in Figure 8.11 (a) and (b) are equivalent.
In some trials, the objective is to Ô¨Ånd the dose that is safe and has the
highest eÔ¨Écacy rate. This can be implemented by (1) setting œÖ11 = 100 and
œÖ00 = 0 in the utility table, or (2) set w = 0 in the toxicity‚ÄìeÔ¨Écacy probability
trade-oÔ¨Äfunction. In both cases, the safety condition is imposed through the
Admissible Criteria, as discussed next.
Admissible Criteria
To safeguard patients from toxic and/or futile doses,
two dose acceptability criteria discussed in Section 8.4.2 are used by BOIN12
to determine which doses may be used to treat patients. According to Figure
8.12, the highest acceptable toxicity probability is œÜT = 0.35, and the lowest
acceptable eÔ¨Écacy probability is œÜE = 0.25. Generally, œÜE can take the value
of the target response rate speciÔ¨Åed for a standard phase II trial. Because U-
BOIN considers the toxicity‚ÄìeÔ¨Écacy trade-oÔ¨Ä, the value of œÜT should be set
slightly higher (e.g., 0.05) than the target toxicity rate used in conventional
toxicity-based phase I designs. For example, if 30% is an appropriate target
toxicity rate that the conventional phase I design used, then œÜT = 0.35 is a
reasonable choice for U-BOIN.
For the toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy probability cutoÔ¨Äs, we recommend cT = 0.95
and cE = 0.90, which seem high, but actually are appropriate as their purpose
FIGURE 8.12: Admissible criteria of BOIN12 design.

198
Model-Assisted Bayesian Designs for Dose Finding and Optimization
is to rule out excessively toxic and ineÔ¨Äective doses. Among admissible doses,
the dose assignment rule will allocate patients to the most desirable dose. In
other words, even if the admissible dose set includes some doses that are not
particularly safe or eÔ¨Écacious, the design will not assign patients to these
suboptimal doses. Due to the large uncertainty of small sample sizes, using
small values for cT and cE will inadvertently eliminate the doses that are
actually admissible, and thus aÔ¨Äect the operating characteristics of the design.
If a dose is inadmissible due to violation of the safety criterion, this dose and
its higher doses are considered inadmissible. During the trial, only admissible
doses can be used to treat patients, and the doses that are not admissible
should be eliminated from the trial.
After the completion of the speciÔ¨Åcation of design parameters, the de-
sign Ô¨Çowchart (Figure 8.13) and decision tables (Figure 8.14) can be gen-
erated by clicking the ‚ÄúGet Decision Table‚Äù button (see Figure 8.12). In
BOIN12, two decision tables will be generated: the Ô¨Årst one summarizes the

shiny app.

ated by the BOIN12 shiny app.
escalation/de-escalation boundaries for toxicity monitoring, and the second
one is the RDS table that deÔ¨Ånes the desirability of each dose. The decision
tables will be automatically included in the protocol template in Step 3, but
can also be saved as separate csv, Excel, or pdf Ô¨Åles in this step when needed.
Step 2: Run simulation
Operating Characteristics
This step generates the operating characteristics
of the design through simulation, see Figure 8.15. Scenarios used for simulation
should cover various possible clinical scenarios, e.g., OBD located at diÔ¨Äerent
dose levels. The software uses the latent bivariate normal random variables to
generate the joint toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy outcomes. Users need to provide the
true marginal toxicity rate and eÔ¨Écacy rate, as well as the correlation between
toxicity and eÔ¨Écacy to simulate data. Here, the correlation corresponds to the
correlation parameter in the covariance matrix of the latent bivariate normal

distribution. The simulation results (shown in Table 8.8) will be automatically
included as a table in the protocol template in the next step, but can also be
saved as a separate csv or Excel Ô¨Åle if needed.

Software for U-BOIN and TITE-BOIN12
The software for U-BOIN and TITE-BOIN12 has the same layout as BOIN12.
The speciÔ¨Åcation of risk-beneÔ¨Åt trade-oÔ¨Äand admissible criteria follow the
same principle as described above. For U-BOIN, a few extra design param-
eters need to be speciÔ¨Åed, which are mostly related to the Ô¨Årst stage of the
design that focuses on the dose exploration based on toxicity using the stan-
dard BOIN design. As shown in Figure 8.17, the design parameters are similar
to those of BOIN, e.g., apply the 3+3 design run-in and accelerated titration.
Section 3.6 provides detailed discussion on how to choose these design param-
eters. For the Stage I ‚Äúconvergence‚Äù stopping rule, we recommend m1 ‚â•9. In
U-BOIN, patients treated in Stage I roll over to Stage II, thus the Stage II
‚Äúconvergence‚Äù early stopping cutoÔ¨Äm2 should be greater than m1. Roughly
speaking, m2 ‚àím1 represents the maximum number of additional patients we
may expand on each of admissible doses identiÔ¨Åed at the end of Stage I to
optimize the dose. For example, in Figure 8.17, m2 = 24 means treating up to
12 additional patients at OBD and/or admissible doses. The exact meaning of

Finding Optimal Biological Dose
203
m2 ‚àím1 depends on which strategy we use to assign patients in Stage II (e.g.,
pick the winner, adaptive randomization, or equal randomization). Simulation
should be performed to validate and calibrate the values of m1 and m2 to ob-
tain desirable operating characteristics. As described previously, identiÔ¨Åcation
of OBD (or optimizing the dose in general) is substantially more challenging
than identiÔ¨Åcation of MTD. Using an excessively small m1 and m2 leads to
very low power to identify OBD.
